<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="93859cef386d4c26bd1034ab4712a1a6"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93859cef386d4c26bd1034ab4712a1a6">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '60ffde4b-4559-4674-841f-7d59881300b2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '96F0D5D40E83DE8291D480DE0E23183D';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;

                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d81879850" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The unique coordination configuration of single-atom materials (SAMs) allows precise reaction control at atomic-level and a potential of unusual electrochemical reaction. Nevertheless, it is a big challenge to prepare main group element with high loading content. Here, multifield-regulated synthesis (MRS) technology is utilized to rapidly produce single-atom antimony (Sb) metal with a high loading of 15 wt.%. Ab initio molecular dynamics simulations reveal the significantly enhanced reaction kinetics of Sb and nitrogen-doped graphene by multi-physics field coupling. Compared with common metallic Sb nanoparticles, atomically dispersed Sb displays remarkably improved electrochemical reaction kinetics and stable structure due to the negligible variation of stresses and volume expansion during the pseudocapacitive alloying-dealloying process. Such extraordinary alloying reaction in well-dispersed Sb atoms enabling homogeneous ion flow can serve as active nucleation sites for regulating even Na metal nucleation and growth. As a result, copper foil coated with only ≈3&nbsp;µm thickness of such material exhibits a high Coulombic efficiency of up to 99.99%, an ultra-low overpotential of 3&nbsp;mV, and a long lifetime exceeding 2500&nbsp;h in symmetrical cells. Furthermore, an anode-free MRS-SbSA||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> battery is constructed, which demonstrates exceptionally high energy density (≈362&nbsp;Wh Kg<sup>−1</sup>), outstanding rate capability and good cycling stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403432-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion battery (SIBs) emerges as a promising alternative in the post-lithium-ion battery era due to their cost-effectiveness and the abundance of sodium resources. However, the practical deployment of SIBs faces challenges stemming from their relatively low energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0001_R_d81879842e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0004_R_d81879842e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With an operating potential as low as −2.71&nbsp;V and a specific capacity reaching 1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, metallic sodium significantly surpasses the capacity of current commercial hard carbon anode (e.g., 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Moreover, sodium metal batteries (SMBs) display promising applications in various emerging battery systems, including Na-S/Se/Te, Na-O<sub>2</sub>, Na-CO<sub>2</sub>, and Na-Br/I<sub>2</sub> battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0005_R_d81879842e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0008_R_d81879842e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, during repetitive plating/stripping cycles, the electrodeposition of metallic sodium often leads to morphological and chemical instabilities, such as the formation of sodium dendrites, capacity decay, short circuits, and even explosions, posing technical obstacles to the practical utilization of metallic sodium anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0009_R_d81879842e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Rev.">9</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0011_R_d81879842e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Joule">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Controlling the heterogeneous nucleation by incorporating sodiophilic metal additives, has been considered as one of the most effective strategies to regulate the Na metal growth and mitigate dendrite-induced catastrophic issues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0012_R_d81879842e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0015_R_d81879842e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cui et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0012_R_d81879842e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> initially proposed seed nucleation in lithium metal anodes to achieve spatially restricted deposition, such strategy has been applied to regulate sodium electrodeposition by various metals, such as Au, Mg, Zn, Sn, Sb, Bi, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0016_R_d81879842e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0020_R_d81879842e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Sci. Adv">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While these sodiophilic metals or composites with strong affinity offer active sites for Na deposition by alloying reactions, they nonetheless cause sodium consumption through reactions such as adsorption, irreversible alloying or transformation reactions prior to electrodeposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0021_R_d81879842e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0023_R_d81879842e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the alloying-dealloying process inevitably produce large volume expansion. Unfortunately, these drawbacks have not received adequate attention and discussion.</p>
         
         <p>A captivating approach is by downsizing the metal materials to the atomic scale, named single-atom materials (SAMs), facilitating uniform nucleation sites and efficient dispersal of sodium ion flux and local charge density, thus achieving a homogeneous electric field distribution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0024_R_d81879842e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0026_R_d81879842e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As demonstrated in electrocatalysts, SAMs distinguished by their abundant active sites, exhibit excellent electrochemical activities and stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0027_R_d81879842e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0031_R_d81879842e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with conventional metal nanoparticles, SAMs possess precise atom-level design through distinctive coordination configuration and electronic structure, and can afford ample active sites with reduced reaction barriers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0019_R_d81879842e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Sci. Adv.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0032_R_d81879842e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This exceptional structure allows single-atom materials to capture metal ions via site-specific chemical reactions, thereby uniformly regulating subsequent deposition process in metal anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0033_R_d81879842e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0037_R_d81879842e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, such material has the great potential in alleviating volume fluctuations and electrode structural fatigue during cycling for the alloyed-type electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0038_R_d81879842e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Environ. Sci.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, SAMs still face two challenges in the application of SMBs or other batteries. One is the facile synthesis of SAMs with high loading content and well dispersion in the state-of-art common methods. The other issue is that their electrochemical mechanism is still unclear compared with the bulk or nanoscale materials. For example, the alloying reaction in atomic scale and the regulation mechanism of metal deposition in SAMs remains unclear.</p>
         
         <p>In the traditional heating way with only heat thermal field, the atomic diffusion process is relatively sluggish, which may be difficult for the fast bonding of metal atom to ligating atoms. Recent years, some innovative sintering routes with outstanding kinetics have been developed to rapidly fabricate the materials, such as Joule heating and carbon thermal shock method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0039_R_d81879842e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Science">39</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0040_R_d81879842e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nature">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by the above technology, here, we report a multifield-regulated synthesis (MRS) route including electric, thermal and pressure field to rapidly produce single-atom antimony within several minutes. This multi-physics field coupling synthesis method enables high loadings of single-atom antimony (MRS-SbSA), reaching up to 15 wt.%. Compared to conventional sintering methods with low SAMs content, MRS-SbSA with well-dispersed Sb atoms supplies abundant nucleation sites for Na metal anodes, which is beneficial to the control of Na ions flux and growth of anchored Na metal. Meanwhile, the atomic alloying-dealloying process mitigates the volume expansion and reduce remarkably the mechanical stress, which greatly improve the electrochemical reversibility. As a result, MRS-SbSA||Na half-cell exhibits a very high Coulombic efficiency (CE) ≈99.9% even after 1800 cycles. MRS-SbSA-Na symmetric cell displays an ultra-low overpotential and impressively long cycle life. Furthermore, an anode-free MRS-SbSA||NVP battery demonstrates exceptionally high energy density and outstanding rate capability. Our findings provide an insightful view of synthesizing high-loading metal single-atom and its unusual behavior and application in rechargeable battery.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403432-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403432-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis, Characterization, and Formation Mechanism of MRS-SbSA</h3>
            
            <p>The schematic diagram of the MRS system and the route to fabricate MRS-SbSA are illustrated in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. Initially, the synthesis of precursor for SbSA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) by a wet chemical route and the regular Sb single atoms in traditional tube furnace are according to the previous report,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0041_R_d81879842e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> herein named TF-SbSA. The synthesis of MRS-SbSA was using the same precursor, and heated by a MRS condition. In the absence of antimony salt, the nitrogen-doped carbon material (NC) is obtained after pyrolysis. NC, TF-SbSA, and MRS-SbSA all have lamellar morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). No visible particles of Sb species are observed for MRS-SbSA in the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of three samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) show amorphous and broad diffraction peaks without any crystalline Sb metal species. Aberration corrected-high angle annular dark field-scanning transmission electron microscopy (AC-HAADF-STEM) image and the near atomic resolution element mappings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1c</a>) verifies the uniform distribution of C, N, and Sb of MRS-SbSA. High angle annular dark field-scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1d</a>) reveals numerous atomically dispersed bright spots in MRS-SbSA, providing evidence for the successful preparation of high-density Sb single atoms. The mass content of Sb atoms is identified as 15.1% by inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), proving the high content from the energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS)-determined result (19.63%, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, MRS-SbSA displays a much lower the surface areas (34 m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) than TF-SbSA (148 m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403432-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c9f14397-c039-4b3d-82fc-a45ca05914d7/aenm202403432-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c9f14397-c039-4b3d-82fc-a45ca05914d7/aenm202403432-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c132413-4a18-4994-a2ec-7b5dfedfdb70/aenm202403432-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c9f14397-c039-4b3d-82fc-a45ca05914d7/aenm202403432-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403432-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403432" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis, characterizations, and formation mechanism of MRS-SbSA. a) Schematic diagram of the MRS system and the route to fabricate MRS-SbSA. b) HRTEM image, c) AC-HAADF-STEM elemental mapping analysis, and d) AC-HAADF-STEM image of MRS-SbSA. AIMD simulation of the synthesis process of e) TF-SbSA and f) MRS-SbSA on the single-atom dispersion at 1073 K (C (grey), N (blue), and Sb (red)). g) Optimized structures and charge density difference plots of Sb atom adsorption on N-doped graphene substrate without and with the presence of external electric field.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations were conducted (see Supplementary Methods for details) to reveal the formation origin of single atom under different sintering routes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1e</a> vividly illustrates the gradual dispersion of Sb atoms and the formation of Sb-N<sub>4</sub> with increasing simulation time at 1073 K. It is found only a few weakly attached Sb atoms on the N-doped graphene were formed even after 6 fs under only thermal field conditions. Conversely, the diffusion motion of Sb atoms on the substrate was significantly accelerated with the cooperation of multi-physics field including electric field, high temperature, and additional pressure conditions, and all defects in N-doped graphene are filled by the Sb-N<sub>4</sub> structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1f</a>). This result suggests the remarkably enhance the activity and diffusion kinetics of Sb atoms, thereby promoting more efficient and faster formation of Sb-N<sub>4</sub> bonds. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations about the binding energy and differential charge density distribution of the Sb-N<sub>4</sub> configuration in such two conditions were further performed to reveal this finding (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0001">1g</a>). Under external electric field, the Sb-N binding energy dramatically increased from 1.44 to 3.51&nbsp;eV. Bader charge analysis revealed a substantial enhancement in charge transfer from Sb atoms to N atoms. The charge of the exposed antimony atom decreased from 5 e<sup>−</sup> to 3.92 e<sup>−</sup> in ordinary Sb-N<sub>4</sub>, and further reduced to 3.28 e<sup>−</sup> under the influence of an external field, demonstrating the dramatically enhanced reactivity between antimony and nitrogen atoms. These findings provide deep insights into the mechanism of multifield-regulated sintering route to rapidly fabricate high loading of antimony single atoms.</p>
            
            <p>The composition and chemical state of MRS-SbSA and TF-SbSA were investigated using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements. The presence of C, N, Sb, and O elements is found (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution XPS C 1s spectrum is deconvoluted into C─C (284.8&nbsp;eV), C─N (285.8&nbsp;eV), and C─O (289.3&nbsp;eV) components (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, MRS-SbSA exhibits fewer C─O groups than TF-SbSA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the oxygen functional groups decompose due to the action of heat and electric fields, leading to a decrease in oxygen content.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0042_R_d81879842e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The N 1s spectrum (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2a</a>) reveals the presence of pyridinic N (398.1&nbsp;eV), pyrrolic N (400.2&nbsp;eV), graphitic N (401.4&nbsp;eV), and oxidized N (403.9&nbsp;eV), and the peak near 398.7&nbsp;eV is largely ascribed to Sb-N bonds. MRS-SbSA has a higher concentration of Sb─N groups than TF-SbSA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information), which further indicates that the MRS technology effectively accelerates the formation process of SbSA.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0043_R_d81879842e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Sb 3d spectrum of MRS-SbSA exhibits two peaks at Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> (538.8&nbsp;eV) and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> (529.6&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2b</a>), indicating the existence of positively charged Sb atoms (Sb<sup>δ+</sup>, 0 &lt; δ &lt;&nbsp;3), which are higher than Sb<sup>0</sup> and slightly lower than Sb<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0041_R_d81879842e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0044_R_d81879842e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">44</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0045_R_d81879842e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nat. Commun.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks observed at 531.2 and 532.7&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2b</a>) were assigned to the C─O and C═O bonds in the O 1s orbital, respectively. Raman spectra reveal that both MRS-SbSA and TF-SbSA have the typical D and G band for carbon materials, and they have the similar I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403432-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0a2190a7-aff7-4237-a504-b0c51431bccd/aenm202403432-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a2190a7-aff7-4237-a504-b0c51431bccd/aenm202403432-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b962d3f1-93c6-4c23-8587-91f7cbcf7e49/aenm202403432-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a2190a7-aff7-4237-a504-b0c51431bccd/aenm202403432-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403432-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403432" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Chemical structure and atomic coordination environment of MRS-SbSA. a) XPS N 1s spectra, b) Sb 3d/O 1s spectra, c) Raman spectra of Sb single atom under different preparation conditions. d) XANES spectra and e) Fourier-transformed EXAFS spectra of Sb foil, Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3,</sub> and MRS-SbSA. f)&nbsp;EXAFS fitting curve of MRS-SbSA. g–i) Wavelet transform of Sb foil, Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3,</sub> and MRS-SbSA.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) techniques were employed to elucidate the local coordination environment of MRS-SbSA and TF-SbSA. The absorption edge of MRS-SbSA falls between those of standard Sb foil and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, indicating the presence of Sb<sup>δ+</sup> (0 &lt; δ &lt; 3) with distinctive electronic structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2d</a>), which is consistent with the XPS result. Furthermore, Fourier transform (FT) EXAFS analysis of MRS-SbSA reveals a prominent peak at ≈1.50 Å, corresponding to the Sb-N bonds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). There are no peaks indexed to Sb-Sb or Sb-O as found in Sb foil and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, indicating that Sb atoms are uniformly dispersed on the carbon matrix with Sb-N coordination. We conducted wavelet transform (WT)-EXAFS analysis (with high resolution in both k- and R-space) to further validate the presence of atomically dispersed metallic sites in MRS-SbSA (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0002">2g–i</a>). In comparison with the WT contour plots of Sb foil and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, a prominent WT signal centered at 4.0 Å<sup>−1</sup> originating from the Sb─N contribution is found. This observation suggests the absence of any Sb-derived nanoparticles or metallic clusters. The quantitative fitting results of the EXAFS data indicate a coordination number of 4 for the Sb center and an average Sb─N bond length of 2.05 Å, indicating that the Sb-N<sub>4</sub> structure predominates for single-atom antimony (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The XANES spectrum of TF-SbSA reveals the coordination features of Sb-N<sub>4</sub> configuration similar to MRS-SbSA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), which agrees with the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0041_R_d81879842e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403432-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance of Half/Full Cells</h3>
            
            <p>MRS-SbSA with high-loading single-atom antimony was used to modify the Cu current collector with a quite thin coating layer ≈3&nbsp;µm in order to demonstrate the unusually high activity to regulate sodium deposition. MRS-SbSA||Na half cells were fabricated to investigate the plating/stripping behavior of metallic sodium. MRS-SbSA electrode exhibits a very small nucleation overpotential (NOP) of 4&nbsp;mV at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, significantly lower than that of TF-SbSA||Na (15 mV) and Cu||Na cells (124&nbsp;mV) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). The overpotential for mass transfer control (MTC) of MRS-SbSA||Na is also as low as 9&nbsp;mV. Interestingly, in the absence of Sb single atoms, the NOP and MTC of NC increase to 28 and 155&nbsp;mV, respectively, indicating that the high-loading single-atom antimony sites significantly facilitate metallic sodium electrodeposition by markedly reducing the energy barrier for nucleation. TF-SbSA||Na and bare copper foil showed fluctuating Coulombic Efficiency (CE) after 500 and 100 cycles at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, and their average CE are 97.76% and 85.90%, respectively. In comparison, MRS-SbSA demonstrates a remarkably consistent CE ≈99.99% over 1800 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3c</a>). The voltage gap of the TF-SbSA electrode is 10&nbsp;mV at the 5th cycle, which progressively escalated to 77&nbsp;mV at the 500th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3d</a>). In contrast, the voltage gap of the MRS-SbSA electrode at the 500th cycle is still only 10&nbsp;mV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3e</a>) and remains stable until 1000 cycles, indicating a significant reduction in energy loss and the highly reversible Na plating/stripping. The unsatisfied or suboptimal outcomes for TF-SbSA highlight the necessity of ample nucleation sites for achieving uniform Na deposition. In addition, we have tried a less coating with ≈1.3 um thick (designated as MRS-SbSA-1 um). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), MRS-SbSA-1 um exhibits improved NOP (10 mV) and MTC (34 mV) compared with the pristine Cu foil and TF-SbSA, but its performance is slightly inferior to that of the 3-um-thick MRS-SbSA coating. This result suggests the coating with suitable thickness is necessary to provide enough active sites. Generally, elevated current density typically leads to a higher likelihood of dendrite formation and low CE. Notably, the MRS-SbSA electrode maintains an average CE of 99.95% over 1200 cycles at 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, even at a substantial plating capacity of 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, it exhibits an exceptionally high CE of ≈99.96% up to 500 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Such excellent performance prompts us to investigate its critical capability. Remarkably, MRS-SbSA maintains a stable CE of nearly 99.99% from 1 to 50&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a constant plating capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3f</a>), but TF-SbSA electrode fails at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and lose cycling life. These results highlight the outstanding performance of high loading single-atom antimony in regulating the uniform plating and stripping even at high capacity and current density.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403432-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1bbec2f2-bcc0-453f-b73c-fe67ba77fb43/aenm202403432-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1bbec2f2-bcc0-453f-b73c-fe67ba77fb43/aenm202403432-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cba4f866-7ceb-440b-bda6-30067b75801b/aenm202403432-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1bbec2f2-bcc0-453f-b73c-fe67ba77fb43/aenm202403432-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403432-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403432" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic voltage profiles of Na plating on different substrates performed with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and b) the corresponding summary of overpotentials. c) Comparison of CE performances of Cu foil, TF-SbSA, and MRS-SbSA. Voltage profiles of Na plating/stripping on (d) TF-SbSA and e) MRS-SbSA at different cycles. f) Stepwise increasing the current of TF-SbSA and MRS-SbSA from 1 to 50&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and back to 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> at 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. In-situ optical microscopy observations of the Na plating process on the (g) bare Cu foil and (h) MRS-SbSA electrodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To illustrate the spatial control of sodium deposition and the effective inhibition of sodium dendrites by MRS-SbSA, in-situ optical microscopy was applied to observe the dynamic plating process of metallic sodium at a constant current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3g</a> and Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), dendritic Na metals appear quickly in 1 min and are rapidly growing on the both sides of bare Cu substrate, accompanying with the dendrites evolving and extending toward the top over time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0046_R_d81879842e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, it is clearly observed Na ions selectively and dispersively nucleate on MRS-SbSA. The surface of MRS-SbSA is uniformly covered by a Na deposition layer free of dendrites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0003">3h</a>; Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The morphological transformation of Cu, TF-SbSA, and MRS-SbSA electrode during the deposition was further investigated through SEM. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), island-like sodium metals can be observed after 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> electrodeposition on pristine Cu foil. In contrast, Na metal can deposit uniformly on the surfaces of TF-SbSA and MRS-SbSA with 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with flat and compact morphology. At a plating condition of 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, only the MRS-SbSA electrode can maintain smooth and dense deposition. In addition, the morphology of the MRS-SbSA electrode remains nearly the same to the initial state even after 100 cycles, indicating high reversibility and an extremely thin SEI layer. In contrast, the dead sodium for the stripped TF-SbSA electrode is found (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). This result is further confirmed by EIS characterizations, which demonstrates the much smaller resistance of MRS-SbSA electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Besides the current density and areal capacity, the Na metal utilization ratio (i.e., depth of discharge, DOD) is another important indicator to evaluate the deposition performance. Achieving long-term durability at high DOD and large capacity remains a big challenge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0047_R_d81879842e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Energy">47</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0049_R_d81879842e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Sci. Adv.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4a</a> displays the voltage-time profiles of the NC-Na||NC-Na and MRS-SbSA-Na||MRS-SbSA-Na cells under a fixed capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (DOD: 83%). The MRS-SbSA-Na symmetric cell shows a low overpotential (≈6&nbsp;mV) at the beginning, increasing to only 10&nbsp;mV even after 2500 h, as indicated by the flat voltage plateaus (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4a</a>). In contrast, the NC-Na symmetric cell can maintain a stable cycle for just over 200 h. Intriguingly, the MRS-SbSA symmetric cell can be steadily cycled with a DOD of up to 83% at a capacity of 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> for over 2000 h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4b</a>). Furthermore, a high DOD of up to 91% was achieved for cycling over 2000 h at 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Additionally, MRS-SbSA-Na displays outstanding rate capability from 0.5 to 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4c</a>). Our record performance and ultrahigh Na utilization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4d</a>) are much superior than the stat-of-art Na metal anodes (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403432-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b960c4d3-9df4-4578-b7df-1e40dfb26848/aenm202403432-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b960c4d3-9df4-4578-b7df-1e40dfb26848/aenm202403432-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c99c42a3-4a2f-4b99-9381-34d23c185ccd/aenm202403432-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b960c4d3-9df4-4578-b7df-1e40dfb26848/aenm202403432-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403432-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403432" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Voltage-time profiles of MRS-SbSA and Cu foil in symmetric cells, the insets show the enlarged curves ≈240, 1200, and 2000 h. b) Voltage-time profiles of MRS-SbSA in symmetric cells at a high deposition capacity. c) Rate performance of symmetrical cells of MRS-SbSA-Na with a deposition capacity of 2&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) The comparison of DOD, capacity, and cycle time with previous works. e) Schematic presentation of the flexible anode-free pouch cell with MRS-SbSA coated Cu current collector. f) Digital photographs of the pouch cell and charge/discharge curves, g) cycling performance of the pouch cell at 1&nbsp;C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further evaluate the practicability of the MRS-SbSA coated Cu substrate with a very thin layer ≈3&nbsp;µm in Na metal battery, an anode-free battery was constructed by directly coupling with commercial NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode without pre-plating Na metal on the anode side (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The MRS-SbSA||NVP battery delivers improved capacity of 111.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C and exhibits significantly superior rate capability, maintaining a high capacity of 94.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, MRS-SbSA||NVP demonstrates excellent cycling stability and high CE at 1 C rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, by considering the cathode mass and very small loading anode of 3&nbsp;µm layer, this anode-free battery configuration achieves an exceptional energy density of ≈362&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, a pouch cell powering a light-emitting diode (LED) validates the practicality of such SBM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4e</a> and inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4f</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0004">4f,g</a>, the MRS-SbSA||NVP maintains a capacity retention rate of 83% after 100 cycles at 1C. The energy density of the pouch cell reaches up to 306&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of cathode and MRS-SbSA coating layer. This result strongly demonstrates the application potential of MRS-SbSA to modify copper substrate for regulating electrodeposition. The concept of anode-free battery is an emerging novel and fascinating strategy for constructing high-energy-density batteries, which is a highly powerful route to conquer the technical shortcoming in developing high energy density Na-based battery.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403432-sec-0050-title">2.3 Underlying Role of Atomically Dispersed Sb</h3>
            
            <p>Metallic Sb nanoparticles (SbNPs) were synthesized as a control experiment (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) to elucidate the crucial role of atomically dispersed Sb. The ex-situ XRD were used to study the reaction mechanisms of MRS-SbSA and SbNPs electrodes during the sodiation/desodiation process (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5a</a>). When the SbNPs is discharged to 0&nbsp;V, the presence of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and NaSb suggests that the alloying reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and Sb undergoes a sequential phase transformation (Sb → NaSb → Na<sub>3</sub>Sb).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0050_R_d81879842e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon can be attributed to slow alloying kinetics, resulting in an incomplete alloying reaction between NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0051_R_d81879842e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, no any crystalline diffraction peaks of alloyed phase were observed for the MRS-SbSA electrode, and there is only a very broad peak at around twenty degrees. This result implies an atomically alloying reaction for MRS-SbSA. Ex-situ XPS spectra for the electrode, obtained after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching to remove the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer, also prove this finding. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5b</a>, the high-resolution Sb 3d spectrum of the SbNPs electrode showed a redshift after sodiation due to the formation of alloy (2Sb + 6Na<sup>+</sup> + 6e<sup>−</sup> → 2Na<sub>3</sub>Sb). The Sb 3d peak can return back approach to the metallic state after charging to 1&nbsp;V, suggesting the partly reversible alloying reaction. The Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is still found on the surface of the SbNPs electrode during the charge and discharge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the alloying reaction of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is not completely reversible. In contrast, the peak location of Sb 3d for MRS-SbSA electrode is almost unchanged (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23b</a>, Supporting Information), implying the stable structure of single-atom state. In addition, NaF is found on the surface of the both two electrodes (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information), which is the usual constituent of SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0015_R_d81879842e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0052_R_d81879842e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nat. Commun.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403432-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aaf6d207-9720-4e48-bc4d-2ecf2f8c6dbb/aenm202403432-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/aaf6d207-9720-4e48-bc4d-2ecf2f8c6dbb/aenm202403432-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fcbb8d7b-0404-4d63-89a7-d24e09677501/aenm202403432-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aaf6d207-9720-4e48-bc4d-2ecf2f8c6dbb/aenm202403432-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403432-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403432" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD curves of the SbNPs and MRS-SbSA at various states during the initial cycle. b,c) Ex situ XPS at different potentials of SbNPs and MRS-SbSA. d) Cyclic voltammetry scan of SbNPs and MRS-SbSA in a potential range of 0.01–2&nbsp;V. e) The relationship between log i and log v. f) The discharge-charge curves of SbNPs and MRS-SbSA at 1 C. Ex situ TEM images of the g) SbNPs and (h) MRS-SbSA at the initial cycle.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The transient reaction kinetic mechanism of MRS-SbSA and SbNPs electrodes were evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) test within a voltage range of 0.01–2&nbsp;V at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5d</a>). The peaks at 0.45, 0.53, and 0.72&nbsp;V can be attributed to the multistep alloying reactions form Sb to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0053_R_d81879842e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Energy Chem.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Correspondingly, the two anodic peaks at 0.80 and 0.88&nbsp;V are assigned to the gradual dealloying reaction from Na<sub>3</sub>Sb to NaSb and finally to Sb. In the second and third cycle, the peak current of the alloying and dealloying reactions gradually decrease, suggesting the poor reversibility. In contrast, the CV curves of MRS-SbSA electrode are much different from SbNPs. They are well overlapped, and only a pair of highly reversible redox peaks between 0.4–0.7&nbsp;V is found. CV tests at different scan rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information) further confirm the significant improved reversibility of MRS-SbSA, and the electrochemical contributions of such two electrodes were also studied. The corresponding calculated b values of the MRS-SbSA and SbNPs electrodes are 0.85 and 0.58 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5e</a>, see the supporting information for detailed calculations), respectively. This result indicates that MRS-SbSA has a higher pseudocapacitive contribution, which is beneficial for faster sodium ion storage kinetics and improved reversibility. The different electrochemical reaction behavior also can be found from the discharge and charge profiles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5f</a>). Only a slope profile is found for MRS-SbSA electrode without platform related to typical alloy-type reaction like SbNPs. Notably, the alloying or de-alloying peaks for the SbNPs electrode gradually diminish with increasing cycle numbers, suggesting poor reversibility of these reactions. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a</a>, Supporting Information). In stark contrast, the alloying/de-alloying redox peaks for the MRS-SbSA electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27b</a>, Supporting Information) show little attenuation with increasing cycle numbers, suggesting the highly reversibility. Such pair of redox peaks agree with the alloying reaction of atomic Sb metals (Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C and O─Sb─N materials).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0038_R_d81879842e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Environ. Sci.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0032_R_d81879842e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Ex-SEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information), Ex situ TEM, and HRTEM were adopted to reveal the different alloying reactions of the MRS-SbSA and SbNPs electrode. Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Sb metal are found for SbNPs electrode after discharge, proving the typical alloying reaction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5g</a>). However, some Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is still found due to the incomplete desodiation reaction, agreeing with the XRD results. In contrast, Na<sub>2</sub>O and NaF belong to the typical SEI composition are found for MRS-SbSA electrode during discharge and charge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0005">5h</a>), and there is no any metallic Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase. Therefore, it is deduced that single-atom Sb has an extraordinary alloy reaction through atomic sites, which displays an excellent reversibility, robust kinetics and structural stability.</p>
            
            <p>Generally, alloy-type Sb metal undergoes a significant volume expansion (≈293%) during the sodiation process, which easily leads to the pulverization of the electrode and poor cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0054_R_d81879842e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Energy Environ. Sci.">54</a>-<a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0056_R_d81879842e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Small">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, downsizing Sb metal to an atomically dispersed state can effectively mitigate the volume change and stabilize the interphase during alloying and de-alloying reaction. Consequently, such material, used as active material to regulate Na metal deposition, has an advantage combining high sodiophility and stable structure as persistent nucleation site. As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6a</a>, such unique structure of single-atom Sb enables the ultralong durability with record-high Na utilization and large areal capacities. The low reversibility and large volume expansion of nanoparticles would also cause the low initial coulomb efficiency (ICE) and poor electric contact, as evidenced in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6b</a>. A serious overcharging at above 0.7&nbsp;V is found for SbNPs electrode with a low ICE of only 74.1%, while MRS-SbSA is as high as 85.4%. This phenomenon is also found in alloy-type Sn metal in regulating the Na metal deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0049_R_d81879842e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Sci. Adv.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such low ICE will consume a consider amount of Na during the first plating procedure, and finally SbNPs electrode shows a fluctuate CE during cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). On the other hand, MRS-SbSA can provide remarkably more active sites for capturing sodium and the subsequent electrodeposition, as evidenced in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6c</a>. At the same magnification and area of EDS mapping, the well distributed Sb single atoms provide much more sites for sodiation than Sb metal, enabling the more abundant and even nucleation sites. While the Sb metal nanoparticles only has limited nucleation sites, and then is easy to form deposition hot spots in certain local areas during the sodium deposition process, thereby triggering dendrite growth and unstable interface reactions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403432-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0f828033-57e4-4423-b653-ac0e0ad10199/aenm202403432-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0f828033-57e4-4423-b653-ac0e0ad10199/aenm202403432-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/51a12efc-8613-4b11-b804-e9862e97f6c5/aenm202403432-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0f828033-57e4-4423-b653-ac0e0ad10199/aenm202403432-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403432-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403432" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of different Na plating behavior on SbNPs and MRS-SbSA electrode. b) Voltage-capacity profiles of SbNPs and MRS-SbSA electrode under cutoff potentials of 1&nbsp;V at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Corresponding EDS elemental mapping Na and Sb after sodiation. The geometry of the SbNPs (d<sub>1</sub>) and MRS-SbSA (e<sub>1</sub>) electrodes for the FEA simulation, and the corresponding sodium concentration distribution (d<sub>2</sub>, e<sub>2</sub>) and stress distribution (d<sub>3</sub>, e<sub>3</sub>). The insets exhibit a cut plane where the sodium concentration and stress were simulated and plotted.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The morphological changes of SbNPs and MRS-SbSA electrodes during the plating/stripping cycles were further investigated to elucidate the improved performance. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information) of the cross-sectional view SEM images, a more compact Na plating layer is found on MRS-SbSA electrode, and the thickness of it is nearly not changed even after 100 cycles, suggesting highly reversible sodium deposition process and high structural reversibility. On the contrary, SbNPs has a bit increased thickness, suggesting a thick SEI layer or some residual and irreversible sodium (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a>, Supporting Information). The EDS element mapping of MRS-SbSA electrode after 50 cycles deposited with 1&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−2</sup> still show the even distribution of Na and Sb (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information). Finite element analysis (FEA) was adopted to simulate the ion concentration distribution and stress distribution of SbNPs and MRS-SbSA electrodes during the sodiation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information). Symmetric boundary conditions were employed in FEA to reduce computational load, resulting in one-eighth modeling of the electrode. The concentration gradient and fluctuation distribution of Na<sup>+</sup> for SbNPs electrode can be clearly observed after sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6d2</a>). This result will cause obvious concentration polarization and inhibit the transport and adsorption process of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0057_R_d81879842e1274" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Eng. J.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, Na ions can be effectively utilized with homogeneous concentration distribution for MRS-SbSA electrode after sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6e2</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0057_R_d81879842e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Eng. J.">57</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0058_R_d81879842e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result demonstrates the even distribution of Na<sup>+</sup> ions flux for MRS-SbSA at the nucleation site, which significantly facilitate the uniform sodium deposition. Furthermore, the electrochemo-mechanical stresses of the different substates were also studied. It is found pristine Sb ball exhibits a significant internal stress over 20&nbsp;GPa after sodiation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33c</a>, Supporting Information), particularly concentrated at the reaction front, leading to ball fracture and electrode failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0038_R_d81879842e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Environ. Sci.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0059_R_d81879842e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Matter">59</a>, <a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0060_R_d81879842e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Joule">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, coupling the Sb ball with the carbon sheet markedly reduces the internal stress near the reaction front to ≈7&nbsp;GPa (65% compared to a single Sb ball) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6d3</a>). This reduction is attributed to stress dispersion from the reaction front to the Sb ball and surrounding carbon sheet interface. Downsizing Sb spheres into atomic Sb can remarkably alleviate the internal stress, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403432-fig-0006">6e3</a>. Notably, the internal stress distribution of MRS-SbSA is uniform across the carbon sheet with minimal stress of ≈2&nbsp;× 10<sup>−7&nbsp;</sup>GPa. Collectively, these findings underscore the beneficial structural characteristics of atomically dispersed Sb in regulating the Na<sup>+</sup> flux distribution and keeping the stable structure. This can be responsible for the excellent Na metal deposition performance toward the practical application level with very thin coating layer, and thus enhancing the overall battery energy density.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403432-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, we first demonstrate the rapid synthesis of a high-loading single-atom Sb through a special heating route by multi-physics field coupling. It is revealed that the diffusion kinetics of Sb metal atoms and their formation of Sb-N bond can be dramatically enhanced by such sintering route. Such material exhibits excellent performance in regulating the Na metal electrodeposition due the ample active sites, stable and highly reversible capacitive alloying/de-alloying reaction. This extraordinary alloying reaction in atomic scale can effectively regulate the even Na ion flux, allowing the tiny nucleation barrier and the homogenous nucleation and growth of Na metal anode. The MRS-SbSA||Na half-cell maintains over 99.9% CE after 1800 cycles, while the MRS-SbSA-Na symmetric cell shows a low overpotential of 3&nbsp;mV and a long cycle life of 2500 h. An anode-free MRS-SbSA||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> battery built from this material has a high energy density of ≈362&nbsp;Wh Kg<sup>−1</sup>, excellent rate capability and good cycling performance. This study provides a fundamental view to fabricate high-loading single-atom materials and demonstrates the extraordinary electrochemical performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403432-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403432-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202403432-sec-0100-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The Sb precursor and comparative sample of single-atom Sb obtained from tube furnace (TF), was prepared from the reported literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0041_R_d81879842e1350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 50.0&nbsp;mg antimonous chloride (SbCl<sub>3</sub>), 1.0&nbsp;g dicyandiamide (DCDA), and 0.1&nbsp;g trimesic acid (H<sub>3</sub>BTC) were dissolved in 5&nbsp;mL of ethanol under ultrasound for 5&nbsp;min, and then kept at 80 °C for 12&nbsp;h. The obtained white powder was ground and put into the MRS hot pressing sintering furnace at 800 °C for 5 mins. The power dissipation is 3 to 4&nbsp;kW with an output current of 300 ≈ 600 A and a voltage of 3 ≈ 6&nbsp;V, the pressure is 25&nbsp;MPa during the whole heating process. In order to remove any metallic Sb species, the samples were leached in 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> solution at 80 °C for 24&nbsp;h, washed thoroughly with deionized water, and were dried in vacuum at 60 °C for overnight to prepare the final product of MRS-SbSA. The comparative sample synthesized from traditional furnace (TF-SbSA) was obtained at 800 °C for 2h. The preparation process of SbNPs was similar to that of TF-SbSA without the presence of DCDA.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403432-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403432-bib-0041_R_d81879842e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

                  <li data-bib-id="aenm202403432-bib-0004"><span class="bullet">4</span><span class="author">M. Li</span>, <span class="author">J. Lu</span>, <span class="author">Z.
